:A [circle](https://artofproblemsolving.com/wiki/index.php/Circle) is [inscribed](https://artofproblemsolving.com/wiki/index.php/Inscribe) in [quadrilateral](https://artofproblemsolving.com/wiki/index.php/Quadrilateral) ABCD, [tangent](https://artofproblemsolving.com/wiki/index.php/Tangent) to overline{AB} at P and to overline{CD} at Q. Given that AP=19, PB=26, CQ=37, and QD=23, find the [square](https://artofproblemsolving.com/wiki/index.php/Perfect_square) of the [radius](https://artofproblemsolving.com/wiki/index.php/Radius) of the circle.

:Call the [center](https://artofproblemsolving.com/wiki/index.php/Center) of the circle O. By drawing the lines from O tangent to the sides and from O to the vertices of the quadrilateral, four pairs of congruent [right triangles](https://artofproblemsolving.com/wiki/index.php/Right_triangle) are formed.Thus, angle{AOP}+angle{POB}+angle{COQ}+angle{QOD}=180, or (arctan(tfrac{19}{r})+arctan(tfrac{26}{r}))+(arctan(tfrac{37}{r})+arctan(tfrac{23}{r}))=180.Take the tan of both sides and use the identity for tan(A+B) to get [tan(arctan(tfrac{19}{r})+arctan(tfrac{26}{r}))+tan(arctan(tfrac{37}{r})+arctan(tfrac{23}{r}))=ncdot0=0.]Use the identity for tan(A+B) again to get [frac{tfrac{45}{r}}{1-19cdottfrac{26}{r^2}}+frac{tfrac{60}{r}}{1-37cdottfrac{23}{r^2}}=0.]Solving gives r^2=boxed{647}.Note: the equation may seem nasty at first, but once you cancel the rs and other factors, you are just left with r^2. That gives us 647 quite easily.

So, in this case, a would be 105m, and b = c - 1575.But since the problem states that "the other leg is less than 1991", so if we assume that the leg which is 1575 less is b, then the other leg a must be less than 1991. So, if we let b = c - 1575, then the other leg a = 105m, and we have a < 1991. So, 105m < 1991, so m < ~18.96, same as before. So, m can be 1 to 18. But since c must be an integer, similar to before, when we set ( 2c = 7m + 1575 ), so again, m must be odd.But in this case, the leg a = 105m, so a must be less than 1991. So, same as before, m 18, but m must be odd, so same m=1,3,...,17.But then, in this case, c = (7m + 1575)/2. Then, b = c - 1575 = (7m + 1575)/2 - 1575 = (7m + 1575 - 3150)/2 = (7m - 1575)/2.But b must be positive. So, 7m - 1575 > 0 => 7m > 1575 => m > 1575/7 => m > 225 => m > 15.Therefore, m must satisfy m > 15. Since m is odd and m 17, then m=17.So, again, m=17. Therefore, c = (7289 + 1575)/2 = (2023 + 1575)/2 = 3598/2 = 1799. Then, b = 1799 - 1575 = 224. a = 10517 = 1785. So, the leg that is 1575 less is b=224 = 1799 - 1575, which is correct, and the other leg a=1785 is less than 1991. So, this also satisfies the problem's conditions. Therefore, regardless of which leg we take as being 1575 less, the hypotenuse is the same: 1799. Therefore, the answer is 1799.So, even when we considered both cases (either a or b being the leg that is 1575 less), the only valid solution is when m=17, leading to hypotenuse c=1799. We need to find the length of the hypotenuse.Let the legs be (a) and (b), and the hypotenuse be (c). Without loss of generality, assume one leg (a = c - 1575). Using the Pythagorean theorem:[a^2 + b^2 = c^2]Substituting (a = c - 1575):[(c - 1575)^2 + b^2 = c^2]Expanding and simplifying:[c^2 - 2 cdot 1575 cdot c + 1575^2 + b^2 = c^2][b^2 = 2 cdot 1575 cdot c - 1575^2][b^2 = 1575(2c - 1575)]Since (b) must be an integer, (1575(2c - 1575)) must be a perfect square. The prime factorization of 1575 is (3^2 times 5^2 times 7). For (1575(2c - 1575)) to be a perfect square, (2c - 1575) must be a multiple of 7. Let (2c - 1575 = 7k^2) for some integer (k):[2c = 7k^2 + 1575][c = frac{7k^2 + 1575}{2}]For (c) to be an integer, (7k^2 + 1575) must be even. Since 1575 is odd, (k) must be odd. Let (k = 2m + 1) (odd). However, we can directly consider (k) as an odd integer. Given (b = 105k) (from the factorization) and (b < 1991):[105k < 1991 implies k < frac{1991}{105} approx 18.96]Thus, (k) can be 1, 3, 5, ..., 17. :[Solution] Let the lengths of the legs be a, b, and the hypotenuse be c, where a, b, and c are positive integers. The problem is:If distinct digits D, E, L, M, Q (each between 0 and 9 inclusive) satisfy the addition:E L+M E M---------Q E DWhat is the maximum possible value of the three-digit integer QED?First, let's make sure I understand the problem correctly. We have a vertical addition here. The rightmost digits (units place) are L + M, which should result in D (with a possible carryover). The middle digits (tens place) are E + E, which should result in E again (with a possible carryover from the units place). The leftmost digits (hundreds place) are the carryover from the tens place plus M, which should result in Q (since there's nothing above the M in the top number). All digits D, E, L, M, Q are distinct, and we need to find the maximum possible value of QED.Our goal is to maximize QED, which is a three-digit number with hundreds digit Q, tens digit E, and units digit D. So, to maximize QED, we want Q as large as possible, then E as large as possible, and then D as large as possible, given the constraints.Let me start by analyzing the columns from right to left, as we usually do in addition problems.Units Column (Rightmost): L + M = D or L + M = D + 10 (if there's a carryover)Since L and M are digits (0-9), their sum can be between 0+0=0 and 9+9=18. However, since all digits must be distinct, L and M cannot be the same. Also, D must be a single digit, so if there's a carryover, L + M would be D + 10. Let's note that possibility.Tens Column (Middle): E + E + carryover from units = E + 10 * carryover to hundredsWait, the middle column is E (from the top number) plus E (from the middle number's tens place) plus any carryover from the units column. The result is E in the tens place of the sum, which is QED. So:E + E + carryover_from_units = E + 10 * carryover_to_hundredsSimplifying this:2E + carryover_from_units = E + 10 * carryover_to_hundredsSubtract E from both sides:E + carryover_from_units = 10 * carryover_to_hundredsSo, E + carryover_from_units must be a multiple of 10. Since carryover_from_units can be 0 or 1 (because the maximum sum in the units column is 9 + 9 = 18, so carryover can be 0 or 1), then E must be either 0 or 9. Wait, let's think:If carryover_from_units is 0, then E + 0 = 10 * carryover_to_hundreds. But E is a digit (0-9), so 10 * carryover_to_hundreds must be 0 or 10. If it's 0, E must be 0. If it's 10, E must be 10, which is impossible. Therefore, if carryover_from_units is 0, E must be 0, but carryover_to_hundreds would also be 0.If carryover_from_units is 1, then E + 1 = 10 * carryover_to_hundreds. Since carryover_to_hundreds must be an integer (0 or 1, because the maximum sum in the tens column is 9 + 9 + 1 = 19, but in our case, the tens column is 2E + 1. We need to determine the maximum number of sides of quadrilateral ( mathrm{ANMK} ) that can have a length of 1.1. Properties of Medians and Centroid: - The centroid ( mathrm{M} ) divides each median into a ratio of 2:1. - ( mathrm{AN} ) is half of ( mathrm{AB} ), ( mathrm{KA} ) is half of ( mathrm{AC} ), ( mathrm{NM} ) is one-third of ( mathrm{CN} ), and ( mathrm{MK} ) is one-third of ( mathrm{BK} ).2. Coordinate System and Equations: - Placing ( mathrm{A} ) at ( (0, 0) ), ( mathrm{B} ) at ( (2b, 0) ), and ( mathrm{C} ) at ( (2c, 2d) ). - Midpoints ( mathrm{N} ) and ( mathrm{K} ) are ( (b, 0) ) and ( (c, d) ), respectively. - Centroid ( mathrm{M} ) is ( left( frac{2b + 2c}{3}, frac{2d}{3} right) ).3. Key Equations: - ( mathrm{AN} = b ) - ( mathrm{NM} = frac{1}{3} sqrt{(-b + 2c)^2 + (2d)^2} ) - ( mathrm{MK} = frac{1}{3} sqrt{(c - 2b)^2 + d^2} ) - ( mathrm{KA} = sqrt{c^2 + d^2} )4. Checking Combinations: - Setting all four sides to 1 leads to degenerate triangles. - Setting three sides to 1 also leads to degenerate triangles. - Setting two sides to 1 (e.g., ( mathrm{AN} = 1 ) and ( mathrm{KA} = 1 )) can be achieved in a non-degenerate triangle, such as an isosceles triangle with ( mathrm{AB} = 2 ), ( mathrm{AC} = 2 ), and ( mathrm{BC} = 2 ).5. So AB + AC=4=BC, which is degenerate.Therefore, even with coordinate geometry, setting AN=1, KA=1, NM=1 leads to degenerate triangle.Thus, no way to have three sides.Therefore, the maximum number is 2.Final AnswerThe maximum number of sides of quadrilateral ANMK that can have a length of 1 is boxed{2}.</think>In triangle ( mathrm{ABC} ), medians ( mathrm{BK} ) and ( mathrm{CN} ) intersect at the centroid ( mathrm{M} ). We need to determine the maximum number of sides of quadrilateral ( mathrm{ANMK} ) that can have a length of 1.1. Properties of Medians and Centroid: - The centroid ( mathrm{M} ) divides each median into a ratio of 2:1. - ( mathrm{AN} = frac{1}{2} mathrm{AB} ), ( mathrm{KA} = frac{1}{2} mathrm{AC} ). - ( mathrm{NM} = frac{1}{3} mathrm{CN} ), ( mathrm{MK} = frac{1}{3} mathrm{BK} ).2. Key Equations: - For ( mathrm{AN} = 1 ), ( mathrm{AB} = 2 ). - For ( mathrm{KA} = 1 ), ( mathrm{AC} = 2 ). - For ( mathrm{NM} = 1 ), ( mathrm{CN} = 3 ). - For ( mathrm{MK} = 1 ), ( mathrm{BK} = 3 ).3. Checking Degenerate Triangles: - Setting ( mathrm{AB} = 2 ), ( mathrm{AC} = 2 ), and medians ( mathrm{BK} = 3 ), ( mathrm{CN} = 3 ) leads to a degenerate triangle with ( mathrm{BC} = 4 ).4. Non-Degenerate Triangles: - Considering different pairs of sides being 1: - ( mathrm{AN} = 1 ) and ( mathrm{KA} = 1 ) (non-degenerate triangle with ( mathrm{AB} = 2 ), ( mathrm{AC} = 2 ), and other sides calculated). - Other pairs like ( mathrm{AN} = 1 ), ( mathrm{MK} = 1 ) or ( mathrm{NM} = 1 ), ( mathrm{MK} = 1 ) also lead to non-degenerate triangles but only two sides of length 1.5. Conclusion: - Any attempt to have three sides of length 1 results in a degenerate triangle. - The maximum number of sides of quadrilateral ( mathrm{ANMK} ) that can be 1 is 2.[boxed{2}]]

:Method 1. Suppose that at least three sides of the quadrilateral ANMK are equal to 1. There are only two fundamentally different cases. 1) mathrm{AN}=mathrm{NM}=mathrm{MK}=1. Then mathrm{NB}=1, mathrm{MB}=2, so mathrm{MN}+mathrm{NB}=mathrm{MB}. 2) mathrm{KA}=mathrm{AN}=mathrm{NM}=1. Then mathrm{AC}=2, mathrm{NC}=3, so mathrm{NA}+mathrm{AC}=mathrm{NC}. In both cases, a contradiction with the triangle inequality is obtained.Method 2. If more than two sides of the quadrilateral are equal to 1, then either mathrm{AK}=mathrm{NA} or mathrm{KM}=mathrm{MN}. In the first case, triangle ABC is isosceles. In the second case, mathrm{BK}=mathrm{CN}=3, so in this case, triangle ABC is also isosceles. From this, it follows that mathrm{AK}=mathrm{KM}=mathrm{MN}=mathrm{NA}, meaning that AKMN is a rhombus. This is a contradiction, as lines AK and NM are not parallel.Method 3. Let mathrm{L} be the midpoint of side mathrm{BC}. If mathrm{NM}=mathrm{NA}=mathrm{NB}, then triangle AMB is a right triangle.Triangle LMK is similar to triangle AMB with a similarity ratio of 0.5, so MK =mathrm{LM}. Therefore, in triangle AMK, the hypotenuse is larger than in triangle LMK, meaning mathrm{AK}>mathrm{LK}=mathrm{NA}. Thus, in the quadrilateral ANMK, there are no three sides equal to each other.The case when mathrm{KM}=mathrm{KA} is analogous, and if mathrm{KM} neq mathrm{KA} and mathrm{NM} neq mathrm{NA}, then among the segments NM, NA, KM, and KA, there are also no three equal to each other.## Answer2 sides.

Part (a)1. Parity Considerations: - The area of the rectangle is 380, which is even. Each odd square has an area congruent to 1 mod 4, and each even square has an area congruent to 0 mod 4. The total number of odd squares must be a multiple of 4. - Using a checkerboard argument, each odd square creates an imbalance of 1 in the count of black and white squares. The total imbalance must be zero, requiring an even number of odd squares. Combined with the modulo 4 condition, the minimal number of odd squares must be 4.2. Although explicit tiling wasn't constructed, the parity arguments confirmed that 4 is the minimal number.Answer for Part (a):[boxed{4}] Part (b)1. :## Solution and grading criteria:a) If we denote by k the number of square pieces obtained and by x_{1}, x_{2}, ldots, x_{k} their dimensions, then writing the area of the rectangle in two ways, we get 19 cdot 20=x_{1}^{2}+x_{2}^{2}+ldots+x_{k}^{2}. Since 19 cdot 20=380 is divisible by 4, the square of an even number is a multiple of 4, and the square of an odd number is a number that gives a remainder of 1 when divided by 4, we deduce that the number of square pieces of odd dimension must be a multiple of 4. ...1p Note further that this number cannot be 0: the side of length 19 cannot be composed of only squares of even dimension. (Another argument: if we color the piece of paper in columns, alternately with white and black such that the first and last columns are black, we would have 20 more black squares than white ones, and a square of even dimension occupies the same number of white and black squares, so we cannot have only these.) 1 p An example with 4 squares of odd dimension is easy to give: for example, cutting from the last 5 columns 4 pieces 5 times 5 (these are the 4 squares of odd dimension). The remaining rectangular piece 20 times 14 can be cut into pieces 2 times 2. 1pb) We color the piece of paper in columns, alternately with white and black such that the first and last columns are black. We will have 20 more black squares than white ones, and a square of even dimension occupies the same number of white and black squares. A square of odd dimension ell occupies either ell more white squares than black ones or ell more black squares than white ones. Therefore, the sum of the dimensions of the squares that cover more black squares than white ones must be 20 more than the sum of the dimensions of the squares that cover more white squares than black ones. It follows that the sum of the dimensions of the squares that cover more black squares than white ones must be at least 20, so the sum of the dimensions of the squares of odd dimension must be at least 20, thus the sum of the perimeters of the odd squares is at least 80. ........ 3p An example in which the required sum is exactly 80 is the one given in part a). .......................1p
